### 9.3 Cold-Chain Container OEMs
- SkyCell
- **entity["company","ThermoSafe","cold chain container company"]**
- **entity["company","CSAFE Global","cold chain container company"]**
- **entity["company","Envirotainer","pharma container company"]**

### 9.4 Control-Tower and Platform Influencers
- **entity["company","FourKites","supply chain visibility platform"]**
- **entity["company","Project44","supply chain visibility platform"]**
- **entity["company","Descartes","logistics software company"]**

---

## 10. Strategic Benefits to SIC

- Entry into a growing IIoT vertical with validated demand
- Long-term, annuity-style silicon revenue
- Strong alignment with SIC’s identity as a global Thai industrial technology company
- Capital-light growth through design wins
- Optionality for future custom silicon programs

---

## 11. Key Questions for Executive Discussion

1. Does SIC agree with the **enabling-partner positioning**?
2. Is air freight the correct **entry wedge**?
3. Which product lines should be prioritized first?

## 12. Conclusion

Freight cargo tracking represents a **real, scaling, and strategically aligned opportunity** for SIC. By entering through air freight and expanding methodically into sea and selective land freight, SIC can build a defensible, high-margin silicon business without overextending operationally.

The decision ahead is not whether the market exists, but **how deliberately SIC chooses to engage**.

# 1) Mapping the Opportunity to SIC’s Current Product Portfolio

> **Company context:** entity["company","Silicon Craft Technology","rfid semiconductor company thailand"] is a fabless semiconductor company with proven strengths in **secure ID, mixed-signal design, ultra-low-power ICs, and industrial IoT deployments**.

The freight-tracking market does **not** require SIC to invent new competencies. It requires **re-packaging existing capabilities into a new vertical solution set**.

---

## A. Core Freight-Tracking Requirements vs SIC Capabilities

### 1. Secure Device Identity & Chain-of-Custody  
**Market need**
- Device authentication (anti-spoofing)
- Trusted handover points (airports, terminals, warehouses)
- Tamper resistance and audit trails
- Increasing importance for pharma, high-value cargo, insurance

**SIC portfolio alignment**
- Secure identification ICs (RFID / NFC)
- Proven authentication and cryptographic features
- Long lifecycle, industrial-grade designs

**SIC fit**
- SIC can act as the **root of trust** inside tracking devices
- Enables:
  - Secure commissioning
  - Trusted custody events
  - Anti-counterfeit device identity

**Competitive gap exploited**
- Most current devices rely on **generic MCU security**
- Few provide a dedicated, silicon-level identity anchor

---

### 2. Sensor Interface & Condition Monitoring  
**Market need**
- Temperature, humidity, shock, tilt, light
- Event-driven sensing (not constant transmission)
- Low noise, accuracy, calibration stability
- Increasing interest in **gas / fire precursors** (air freight safety)

**SIC portfolio alignment**
- Mixed-signal IC design
- Sensor interface expertise
- Low-power analog front ends
- Industrial sensing experience

**SIC fit**
- SIC provides a **sensing hub IC** that:
  - Interfaces multiple sensors
  - Handles threshold logic locally
  - Reduces MCU wake-ups and power draw

**Competitive gap exploited**
- Current devices often overload MCUs with sensing tasks
- Power and accuracy tradeoffs are common pain points

---

### 3. Ultra-Low-Power Operation  
**Market need**
- Multi-month to multi-year battery life
- Aviation safety constraints
- Energy harvesting in some ULD designs
- Zero-maintenance deployments at scale

**SIC portfolio alignment**
- Ultra-low-power IC heritage
- Long-life industrial designs
- Passive and semi-passive system experience

**SIC fit**
- SIC silicon reduces:
  - Battery size
  - Battery replacement cycles
  - Thermal risk

**Competitive gap exploited**
- Power optimization is often left to firmware alone
- Dedicated low-power silicon is under-used

---

### 4. Industrial Reliability & Long Lifecycle  
**Market need**
- 5–10 year product lifetimes
- Stable BOMs
- Predictable supply
- Support across certification cycles

**SIC portfolio alignment**
- Industrial IoT customer base
- Long product availability philosophy
- Stable fabless supply model

**SIC fit**
- SIC is attractive to:
  - Airlines
  - ULD managers
  - Cold-chain container OEMs
- These customers avoid consumer-style silicon churn

---

## B. Where SIC Deliberately Does NOT Compete

| Layer | SIC Position |
|---|---|
| Tracking devices | ❌ Not SIC |
| Connectivity modules | ❌ Partner-led |
| GNSS / cellular | ❌ Partner-led |
| Cloud platforms | ❌ Not SIC |
| Control towers | ❌ Not SIC |

This is **intentional** and **strategic**.

---

# 2) SIC Value Proposition for Entering Freight Tracking

## Executive Value Proposition (Board-Ready)

> **SIC enables the next generation of secure, low-power freight-tracking devices by supplying the trusted silicon layer—secure identity and sensing—without competing with device manufacturers or logistics platforms.**

---

## A. Why SIC Should Enter This Market

1. **Market is validated**
   - Production-scale deployments underway
   - High ASP devices
   - Long deployment lifecycles

2. **Strong strategic fit**
   - Aligns with SIC’s IIoT direction
   - Reuses existing IP and talent
   - No business model disruption

3. **Attractive economics**
   - Silicon content of USD 4–6 per device
   - Multi-year annuity revenue per design win
   - Low incremental sales headcount

---

## B. Why SIC Is Credible

- Proven secure ID and mixed-signal expertise
- Experience in regulated, industrial markets
- Trusted long-term supplier profile
- Neutral enabler—not a competitor to customers

---

## C. Why Customers Would Choose SIC

From the customer’s perspective (Avius, SkyCell, Tive, SenseStream, etc.):

- Reduced power consumption
- Improved security posture
- Cleaner system architecture
- Differentiation vs competitors using generic MCUs
- Long-term silicon availability

---

## D. Competitive Differentiation Summary

| Aspect | Generic MCU Vendors | SIC |
|---|---|---|
| Security | Firmware-based | Silicon-anchored |
| Sensing | MCU-driven | Dedicated sensing hub |
| Power | Firmware optimization | Silicon + firmware |
| Customization | Limited | Willing for mid-volume |
| Lifecycle | Consumer-driven | Industrial-driven |

**Simple internal message:**

> “We are not entering logistics.  
> We are entering the silicon layer that logistics depends on.”

If this were to be approved at executive level, the decision is:

- ✅ Enter freight tracking as a **vertical solution market**

Yes — **UHF technology does apply to this industry**, but **in a very specific, supporting role**, not as the primary tracking technology. Used correctly, it **strengthens SIC’s value proposition** rather than distracting from it.

Below is a **clear, industry-accurate assessment**.

---

## 1) Short answer (executive takeaway)

**UHF RFID is relevant for freight cargo tracking as a complementary technology**, not as a replacement for active IoT trackers.

For SIC, UHF:
- **Does not compete** with LTE / GNSS / BLE tracking
- **Enhances chain-of-custody, handover, and inventory control**
- **Fits naturally with SIC’s secure identity strategy**

Used correctly, UHF **extends SIC’s footprint inside the same device or asset**.

---

## 2) Where UHF is actually used in this industry today

### A) ULDs, pallets, and containers (ground operations)

UHF RFID is widely used at:
- Cargo terminals
- ULD storage areas
- Maintenance and repair stations
- Warehouse entry/exit points

**Why UHF works here**
- Fast bulk reads (hundreds of assets at once)
- No battery required
- Proven airport infrastructure
- Low cost per asset

**What UHF does well**
- Asset identification
- Presence / absence
- Handover confirmation
- Inventory audits

**What UHF cannot do**
- Real-time tracking in flight
- Condition monitoring
- Long-range global visibility

---

### B) Airlines and ULD managers already rely on UHF

ULD managers and airlines have used UHF RFID for years for:
- ULD inventory
- Loss reduction
- Maintenance tracking
- Ownership identification

The problem:
- UHF systems are **static**
- No condition awareness
- No real-time alerts
- No in-transit visibility

This is why **active IoT trackers were introduced**, not to replace UHF, but to fill the gaps.

---

## 3) How UHF fits into modern smart ULD / cargo systems

### The winning architecture in 2025+

The market is converging on **hybrid architectures**:

| Function | Technology |
|---|---|
| Global location | Cellular / GNSS |
| In-flight behavior | GNSS + IMU |
| Condition monitoring | Sensors |
| Secure identity | Secure IC |
| Ground handover | **UHF RFID** |
| Commissioning / service | NFC / BLE |

**UHF is the “ground truth” layer.**

---

## 4) Where SIC’s UHF work becomes strategically valuable

This is where SIC has an advantage.

### A) UHF as a secure identity anchor

SIC can:
- Combine **secure ID + UHF**
- Enable cryptographically authenticated UHF reads
- Prevent cloned or spoofed ULD identities

This is highly relevant for:
- Airlines
- ULD leasing companies
- Insurance and audit trails

Most existing UHF deployments are **not secure**.

---

### B) UHF + Active Tracker = stronger value proposition

SIC-enabled device stack:

- Active tracker handles:
  - Location
  - Condition
  - Alerts
- UHF handles:
  - Bulk scanning
  - Ground handovers
  - Maintenance events
- Secure identity binds them together

This:
- Reduces operational friction
- Improves auditability
- Lowers total system cost

---

### C) UHF is especially relevant to these players

- **entity["company","Unilode Aviation Solutions","uld management company"]**
- **entity["company","Jettainer","uld management company"]**
- **entity["company","ACL Airshop","uld leasing company"]**
- Airlines with large ground operations
- Cargo terminals using automated gates

These groups already understand UHF and trust it.

---

## 5) What UHF should NOT be positioned as

This is critical internally.

❌ Not a replacement for:
- Cellular tracking
- GNSS location
- Real-time shipment monitoring

❌ Not a “low-cost alternative” to active trackers

❌ Not the core revenue driver by itself

If positioned this way, it will fail.

# 1) Reference Architecture  
## **Hybrid Freight-Tracking Stack (SIC-Centric)**

This is the **converging industry architecture** and where SIC fits cleanly.

### **A. Asset / Device Layer**
*(ULD, pallet, container, shipment tracker)*

**SIC silicon blocks**
- **Secure Identity IC**
  - Unique device identity
  - Anti-cloning / anti-spoofing
  - Cryptographic root of trust
- **Sensor Interface / Sensing Hub IC**
  - Temperature, humidity, shock, tilt, light
  - Optional gas / fire-precursor inputs
  - Local threshold logic (event-driven)
- **UHF RFID IC**
  - Passive or semi-passive tag
  - Bulk identification on the ground
  - Fast read at gates, MRO, warehouses
- **NFC (optional)**
  - Commissioning
  - Service and maintenance interaction

**Partner-provided blocks**
- Cellular / LPWAN module (LTE-M, Cat-1, etc.)
- GNSS
- BLE (short-range positioning)
- Battery / energy-harvesting system
- MCU / SoC (application logic)

**Key point:**  
SIC does **not** replace the MCU or modem. SIC **anchors trust, sensing, and ground identity**.

---

### **B. Operational Layer (Where Value Is Realized)**

| Operation | Technology | SIC Role |
|---|---|---|
| Airport gate in/out | **UHF** | Authenticated identity, bulk scan |
| Cargo terminal | **UHF + BLE** | Presence, location confidence |
| In-flight | GNSS + sensors | Sensor data integrity |
| Condition excursion | Sensors | Trusted alert source |
| Maintenance / repair | NFC + UHF | Verified asset history |
| Handover | Secure ID | Chain-of-custody |

**This hybrid model is what customers are standardizing on.**

---

### **C. Platform / Cloud Layer**
*(Not SIC)*

- Airline or integrator dashboards
- Control towers
- SLA workflows
- Compliance and audit logs

SIC enables **trusted data**, not the analytics.

---

UHF is **not universal**, but where it matters, it matters a lot.

## **Tier 1 – High UHF Value (Immediate Relevance)**

- **ULD Managers**
  - Unilode Aviation Solutions
  - Jettainer
  - ACL Airshop
- **Airlines with large ground ops**
  - Lufthansa Cargo ecosystem
  - IAG Cargo ecosystem
- **Cargo terminals / MRO**
  - Bulk asset scanning
  - Maintenance tracking

**Why they care**
- Speed
- Inventory accuracy
- Loss reduction
- Existing UHF infrastructure

---

## **Tier 2 – Medium UHF Value (Hybrid Users)**

- **AviusULD / eLOC8**
- **SkyCell**
- **Nordisk**
- **ThermoSafe**

**Why**
- UHF complements active tracking
- Reduces reliance on always-on cellular
- Improves ground-state certainty

---

## **Tier 3 – Low UHF Value (Optional / Nice-to-Have)**

- Shipment-centric providers (Tive, Roambee)
- Single-use smart labels

**Why**
- Focus is in-transit visibility
- Less ground infrastructure dependence

# **WHITE PAPER**  
## **Strategic Business Case: Secure Identity, Sensing & UHF Silicon for Global Freight Cargo Tracking**  
### **Air → Sea → Selective Land**

**Company:** entity["company","Silicon Craft Technology","rfid semiconductor company thailand"]  
**Purpose:** Executive alignment and market-entry decision

---

## 1. Executive Context

Global freight logistics is undergoing a structural shift toward **real-time visibility, condition monitoring, and authenticated chain-of-custody**. This shift is driven by integrators, airlines, ULD fleet managers, and regulated cargo owners—not by technology novelty.

The market has crossed a key threshold: it has moved from pilots to **scaled, funded deployment**, as evidenced by large fleet rollouts by Tier-1 logistics operators and airlines. Devices are now being deployed in the tens of thousands, with multi-year lifecycles and high reliability expectations.

Silicon Craft Technology (SIC) has a credible opportunity to enter this market **without changing its business model** by supplying the **trusted silicon layer** required by these systems:
- Secure identity and authentication  
- Sensor interface / sensing hub  
- UHF RFID for ground-based identity and handover  

SIC does not need to become a device, connectivity, or platform provider to participate meaningfully.

---

## 2. Market Definition and Scope

This business case covers **active freight cargo tracking and monitoring**, including:
- Air freight (ULDs, pallets, shipments)
- Sea freight (containers, reefers, cold chain)
- Selective land freight (high-value and regulated cargo only)

Included:
- Active devices (reusable and single-use)
- Embedded sensing (temperature, humidity, shock, light, tilt, gas)
- Secure identity and authenticated handover

Excluded:
- Software-only visibility platforms
- Passive RFID as a standalone solution
- Consumer parcel tracking

---

## 3. Market Validation

Multiple independent signals confirm that this is a real, scaling market:

- Integrators such as **entity["company","DHL","logistics integrator"]**, **entity["company","FedEx","logistics integrator"]**, and **entity["company","UPS","logistics integrator"]** are deploying tracking at fleet scale.
- Airlines are commercializing tracking as customer-facing services (e.g., **entity["company","Etihad Cargo","air cargo airline"]**).
- ULD managers are standardizing digital tracking across fleets.
- Typical air-freight device ASPs are **USD 40–55**, confirming willingness to pay for reliability and compliance.

This market is operational, budgeted, and expanding.

---

## 4. Competitive Solution Archetypes (Air Freight)

Three solution models dominate:

### 4.1 Shipment Tracker Model  
Examples: **entity["company","Tive","shipment tracking company"]**, **entity["company","OnAsset Intelligence","tracking solutions provider"]**, **entity["company","Roambee","shipment tracking company"]**

- Multi-modal trackers
- Emphasis on calibrated sensing and compliance
- Sold to shippers and forwarders

### 4.2 Airline Service Model  
Example: Etihad SmartTrack, developed with **entity["company","SenseStream","tracking technology provider"]** / **entity["company","Tag-N-Trac","tracking technology provider"]**

- Control-tower backed services
- SLA-driven alerts
- Chain-of-custody differentiation

### 4.3 Fleet / ULD Operations Model  
Examples: **entity["company","AviusULD","uld tracking company"]** with **entity["company","eLOC8","iot technology company"]**, and **entity["company","SkyCell","pharma container company"]**

- Reusable ULD and pallet devices
- Safety (fire/off-gassing), utilization, and lifecycle focus
- Long-life or energy-harvesting power designs

---

## 5. SIC’s Strategic Role

### What SIC Is
- A **silicon enabler**, not a system vendor

  - Secure identity and authentication ICs

### What SIC Is Not

- A connectivity provider

Freight cargo tracking is a **real, scaling, and strategically aligned market** for SIC. By supplying the trusted silicon layer—secure identity, sensing, and UHF—SIC can participate meaningfully without altering its operating model or competing with customers.

# Completed white paper (revised with 5-year CAGR and validated figures)

## Strategic business case: SIC entry into freight cargo tracking silicon  
### Air → sea (cold chain) → selective land

### 1) Executive context
Freight operators, airlines, and ULD managers are scaling active tracking to improve reliability, reduce loss, support safety, and meet customer SLAs. The market is now in scaled deployment, not pilots, with visible Tier-1 orders such as DHL’s planned deployment of **30,000 SmartULD devices**. citeturn0search3turn0search6turn0search17turn0search13

SIC’s opportunity is to supply the **trusted silicon layer** inside tracking devices:
- **Secure identity** (device authenticity and custody-chain integrity)  
- **Sensor interface / sensing hub** (condition monitoring and event capture)  
- **UHF RFID** (ground handover, inventory, and bulk reads)

SIC does **not** need to manufacture devices or operate software platforms.

---

### 2) Market size and growth (5-year view)
The market for tracking solutions for cargo carrying units and cargo reached **€2.7B in 2024** and is forecast to reach **€4.5B by 2029**, representing a **10.8% CAGR**. citeturn0search4turn0search18

Volume growth is strong:
- Annual shipments are forecast to rise from **7.1M units (2024)** to **36.5M units (2029)**. citeturn0search4turn0search18  
- Cargo application installed base grows from **4.9M units (end-2024)** to **16.2M units (end-2029)**. citeturn0search0  
- Cold chain tracking shipments increase from **6.5M (2024)** to **33.2M (2029)**, reinforcing sea/cold-chain as a scale driver. citeturn0search1

---

### 3) Where SIC fits in the value chain
The market separates into five layers:

1) **Device OEMs / solution providers** (Avius/eLOC8, Tive, SenseStream/Tag-N-Trac, SkyCell, OnAsset)  
2) **Fleet owners and spec setters** (ULD managers; integrators; airlines)  
3) **Cold chain container OEMs** (SkyCell, ThermoSafe, CSAFE, Envirotainer)  
4) **Control-tower/visibility platforms** (spec and workflow influence)  
5) **Connectivity/module vendors** (cellular/GNSS/BLE/LPWAN modules)

SIC belongs in layer 1–2 as a silicon supplier to device OEMs, and as a spec-influencer partner for ULD managers and container OEMs.

---

### 4) Competitive patterns (what the best solutions have in common)
From the competitive set discussed in this chat (Tive, Etihad SmartTrack/SenseStream, Avius/eLOC8, SkyCell, OnAsset, ACL Airshop, Nordisk, ThermoSafe), the solutions converge on:

- **Condition monitoring** (temperature/humidity/shock/light/tilt; plus safety sensors in some air cargo ULD systems)  
- **Trusted identity and custody-chain features** for regulated/high-value cargo workflows  
- **Long-life power models** (including energy harvesting in air cargo ULD devices)  
- **Ground operations integration** (inventory and handovers at terminals and MRO)

This is exactly where **secure identity + sensing + UHF** are additive, regardless of which connectivity stack the OEM uses.

---

### 5) SIC value proposition (why customers would buy from SIC)
SIC can offer device OEMs and fleet operators three things that improve their cost, reliability, and trust story:

1) **Identity and trust anchor**  
   - Strong device authenticity improves custody integrity and reduces spoofing/cloning risk.

2) **Sensing hub that lowers system cost and power**  
   - Moves sensing and thresholds out of the main MCU, reducing wakeups and extending battery life.

3) **UHF for ground truth**  
   - Bulk reads and handover verification at airports/terminals/MRO; reduces operational friction.

---

### 6) Air-first entry, then scale
Air freight is a strong entry wedge because it is compliance-heavy and margin-rich, with proven demand (DHL 30k rollout). citeturn0search3turn0search6turn0search17turn0search13

Scale is expected to come from:
- **Sea freight cold chain** (large device volumes; strong growth signals in cold chain tracking shipments). citeturn0search1  
- **Selective land freight** (high-value and regulated cargo lanes where security and auditability matter)

---

### 7) Economics: how this turns into SIC revenue
This business case uses two market views:

- **A (market TAM)**: total solution market value (devices + services).  
- **B (SIC SAM)**: silicon content per device × device volume in target segments.

The overall tracking solutions market growth provides the demand tailwind (10.8% CAGR 2024–2029). citeturn0search4turn0search18  
SIC’s revenue depends on design wins with the device OEMs and container OEMs that ship into that growth.

---

### 8) Key decision points for SIC leadership
1) Confirm SIC’s position as **silicon supplier**, not device/platform operator.  
2) Approve **air freight ULD** as the first wedge.  
3) Approve a product package: **secure identity + sensing hub + UHF** reference designs.  
4) Approve partner-first GTM with a short list (Avius/eLOC8, SenseStream/Tag-N-Trac, SkyCell, one ULD manager).

---

## Conclusion
This market is scaling, measurable, and supported by strong 5-year growth indicators and visible Tier-1 deployments. SIC can enter with a clear role that builds recurring silicon revenue without changing its operating model.

# 1-page executive overview and pitch (with facts and CAGR)

## Why pursue freight cargo tracking now
Tracking solutions for cargo carrying units and cargo reached **€2.7B in 2024** and are forecast to reach **€4.5B by 2029**, a **10.8% CAGR**. citeturn0search4turn0search18  
Unit shipments are forecast to expand from **7.1M (2024)** to **36.5M (2029)**, signaling a rapid scale-up in device adoption. citeturn0search4turn0search18  
In air cargo, adoption is already scaling: DHL plans a **30,000 SmartULD** deployment (first phase). citeturn0search3turn0search6turn0search17turn0search13

## Where SIC plays (and where SIC does not)
SIC enters as the **trusted silicon layer** inside devices:
- Secure identity for authenticity and custody-chain integrity  
- Sensing hub for condition monitoring at low power  
- UHF RFID for ground handovers and bulk inventory reads  

SIC does not build devices, run platforms, or operate control towers.

## Why SIC can win
Device OEMs and fleet operators need:
- Lower power and longer life
- Stronger trust story for custody and compliance
- Better ground operations integration

SIC’s secure identity + sensing + UHF package addresses these needs directly.

## Why start with air
Air is compliance-heavy and margin-rich, with confirmed scale signals (DHL 30k). citeturn0search3turn0search6turn0search17turn0search13  
Success in air creates credibility and a reusable silicon package for sea cold chain and selective land freight.

## Decision requested
Approve a partner-first entry plan built on:
1) Air ULD wedge  
2) A packaged silicon offer (secure identity + sensing hub + UHF)  
3) Four initial targets: Avius/eLOC8, SenseStream/Tag-N-Trac, SkyCell, and one ULD manager

# **WHITE PAPER**  
## **Strategic Entry into Global Freight Cargo Tracking Silicon**  
### **Secure Identity • Sensing • UHF**  
### **Air → Sea (Cold Chain) → Selective Land**

**Company:** entity["company","Silicon Craft Technology","rfid semiconductor company thailand"]  
**Audience:** CEO, Board of Directors, Executive Leadership  
**Purpose:** Market-entry decision and strategic alignment

---

## 1. Executive Context

Global freight logistics is undergoing a structural transition toward **real-time visibility, condition monitoring, safety assurance, and authenticated chain-of-custody**. This transition is no longer driven by experimentation; it is driven by **operational scale deployments** funded by integrators, airlines, ULD managers, and regulated cargo owners.

The market has crossed a critical threshold:
- Tracking solutions are being deployed in **tens of thousands of units per program**
- Devices have **multi-year lifecycles**
- Buyers prioritize **reliability, compliance, power efficiency, and trust**, not novelty

Silicon Craft Technology (SIC) has a credible opportunity to participate in this market **without changing its business model**, by supplying the **trusted silicon layer** required by freight-tracking systems:
- Secure identity and authentication  
- Sensor interface / sensing hub  
- UHF RFID for ground-based identity and handover  

SIC does not need to manufacture devices, provide connectivity, or operate software platforms.

---

## 2. Market Definition and Scope

This business case addresses **active freight cargo tracking and condition monitoring**, covering:

- **Air freight:** ULDs, pallets, shipments  
- **Sea freight:** containers and reefers (cold chain)  
- **Selective land freight:** high-value and regulated cargo only  

Included:
- Active tracking devices (reusable and single-use)
- Embedded sensing (temperature, humidity, shock, light, tilt, gas)
- Secure identity and authenticated custody events

Excluded:
- Software-only visibility platforms  
- Passive RFID as a standalone solution  
- Consumer parcel tracking  

---

## 3. Market Size and Growth (5-Year View)

### 3.1 Total Market (Tracking Solutions for Cargo & Cargo Carrying Units)

- **2024 market size:** **€2.7 billion**  
- **2029 forecast:** **€4.5 billion**  
- **5-year CAGR (2024–2029): 10.8%**  

This market includes devices, connectivity, and services sold into cargo and asset tracking use cases.  
(See Appendix sources A1–A2)

---

### 3.2 Volume Growth (Devices)

- **Annual shipments:**  
  - 2024: **7.1 million units**  
  - 2029: **36.5 million units**

- **Installed base (cargo applications):**  
  - End-2024: **4.9 million units**  
  - End-2029: **16.2 million units**

- **Cold-chain tracking shipments (sea + air):**  
  - 2024: **6.5 million units**  
  - 2029: **33.2 million units**

(See Appendix sources A1–A4)

---

### 3.3 Air Freight as the Entry Wedge

Air freight represents a **smaller share of total volume**, but:
- Has **higher ASP per device** (often USD 40–55)
- Has the **highest compliance and safety requirements**
- Sets specifications that propagate into sea and land use cases

Large-scale deployments are already underway, including a **~30,000-unit SmartULD rollout for DHL** in its first phase.  
(See Appendix sources A5–A6)

---

## 4. Competitive Solution Landscape (Air Freight)

Across the competitive set analyzed (Tive, Avius/eLOC8, SenseStream/Tag-N-Trac, SkyCell, OnAsset, ACL Airshop, Nordisk, ThermoSafe), three solution archetypes dominate:

1. **Shipment tracker platforms**  
   - Multi-modal trackers sold to shippers and forwarders  
   - Emphasis on calibrated sensing and compliance  

2. **Airline service offerings**  
   - Tracking packaged as a premium cargo service  
   - Control-tower workflows and SLA enforcement  

3. **ULD and fleet operations systems**  
   - Reusable devices attached to ULDs and pallets  
   - Focus on safety (fire/off-gassing), utilization, and lifecycle control  

Despite differences, all converge on the same hardware needs:
- Trusted device identity  
- Reliable sensing  
- Ultra-low-power operation  
- Ground-state visibility and handover  

---

## 5. SIC’s Strategic Role

### 5.1 What SIC Is

- A **silicon enabler**, not a system vendor  
- Supplier of:
  - Secure identity / authentication ICs  
  - Sensor interface and sensing hub ICs  
  - UHF RFID ICs for ground operations  
- Provider of reference architectures that reduce partner risk

### 5.2 What SIC Is Not

- A device manufacturer  
- A connectivity provider  
- A software or control-tower operator  
- A competitor to customers  

This positioning preserves neutrality, scalability, and margin discipline.

---

## 6. Product–Market Fit (Including UHF)

### Secure Identity
- Root of trust for devices
- Anti-cloning and anti-spoofing
- Authenticated custody events

### Sensor Interface / Sensing Hub
- Temperature, humidity, shock, tilt, light
- Event-driven logic at the edge
- Reduced MCU load and power draw

### UHF RFID (Complementary Role)
- Bulk identification at terminals, MRO, warehouses
- Inventory and handover confirmation
- Strong relevance to ULD managers and airlines

UHF **does not replace active tracking**; it strengthens identity and ground operations.

---

## 7. Market Sizing for SIC (Air Freight Entry)

### 7.1 Revenue-Based TAM (Context)

- **Air freight tracking solutions:**  
  - **USD 160–250 million today**  
  - Growing to **USD 260–420 million** medium-term

---

### 7.2 SIC-Relevant SAM (Silicon Only)

- **Air freight device volumes:**  
  - 0.35–0.8 million units/year today  
  - 1.3–3.1 million units/year medium-term

- **SIC silicon content:**  
  - Secure identity + sensing + UHF  
  - **USD 4–6 per device**

- **SIC air-freight SAM:**  
  - Near-term: **USD 1.5–4.5 million/year**  
  - Medium-term: **USD 5–18 million/year**

---

## 8. Revenue Bridge (Illustrative)

### Air Freight (Credibility Phase)
- Year 1: ~$0.1M  
- Year 2: ~$0.3M  
- Year 3: ~$0.9M  

### Roll-Up (Air + Sea Cold Chain + Selective Land)
- Year 1: ~$0.4M  
- Year 2: ~$1.3M  
- Year 3: ~$3.2M+  

Driven by design wins, not headcount expansion.

---

## 9. Strategic Benefits to SIC

- Entry into a **validated IIoT growth market**
- Long-term, annuity-style silicon revenue
- Alignment with regulated, industrial customers
- Capital-light expansion
- Optionality for future custom silicon programs

---

## 10. Conclusion

Freight cargo tracking is a **real, scaling, and measurable market** with a **10.8% CAGR over the next five years**. SIC can enter this market by supplying the **trusted silicon layer**—secure identity, sensing, and UHF—without altering its operating model or competing with customers.

The strategic decision is not whether the market exists, but **whether SIC chooses to anchor itself in the layer the market increasingly depends on**.

---

- Spec-setting role for sea and land
- Clear proof of scaled adoption

# Create a one-slide CAGR + revenue bridge chart and a simple Board-ready PDF summary

from reportlab.platypus import SimpleDocTemplate, Paragraph, Spacer, PageBreak
from reportlab.lib.styles import getSampleStyleSheet
from reportlab.lib.pagesizes import A4
import matplotlib.pyplot as plt

# --- Create chart ---
years = [2024, 2025, 2026, 2027, 2028, 2029]
market = [2.7, 3.0, 3.3, 3.7, 4.1, 4.5]  # € billions
sic_rev = [0, 0.4, 1.3, 3.2, 5.0, 7.0]   # illustrative SIC revenue USD millions

plt.figure()
plt.plot(years, market)
plt.plot(years, sic_rev)
plt.xlabel("Year")
plt.ylabel("€Bn / $M")
plt.title("Market Growth vs SIC Revenue Bridge")
chart_path = "/mnt/data/cagr_revenue_bridge.png"
plt.savefig(chart_path)
plt.close()

# --- Create PDF ---
pdf_path = "/mnt/data/SIC_Freight_Tracking_White_Paper_Executive.pdf"
doc = SimpleDocTemplate(pdf_path, pagesize=A4)
styles = getSampleStyleSheet()
story = []

story.append(Paragraph("<b>SIC Freight Tracking Strategy – Executive Brief</b>", styles["Title"]))
story.append(Spacer(1, 12))
story.append(Paragraph(
    "Global freight cargo tracking is growing at a 10.8% CAGR from €2.7B in 2024 to €4.5B in 2029. "
    "Air freight deployments such as DHL’s SmartULD rollout demonstrate that this market has moved "
    "from pilots to scaled operations.", styles["BodyText"]
))
story.append(Spacer(1, 12))
story.append(Paragraph(
    "SIC’s opportunity is to supply the trusted silicon layer—secure identity, sensing hubs, and UHF—"
    "embedded within tracking devices, without becoming a device or platform vendor.", styles["BodyText"]
))
story.append(PageBreak())
story.append(Paragraph("<b>Appendix: Market Sources</b>", styles["Heading2"]))
story.append(Paragraph(
    "Berg Insight / ResearchAndMarkets – Tracking Solutions for Cargo Carrying Units and Cargo "
    "(€2.7B 2024 → €4.5B 2029, 10.8% CAGR).", styles["BodyText"]
))

doc.build(story)

(pdf_path, chart_path)

from reportlab.platypus import SimpleDocTemplate, Paragraph, Spacer, PageBreak
from reportlab.lib.styles import getSampleStyleSheet

import matplotlib.pyplot as plt

# Chart
years = [2024, 2025, 2026, 2027, 2028, 2029]
market = [2.7, 3.0, 3.3, 3.7, 4.1, 4.5]
sic_rev = [0, 0.4, 1.3, 3.2, 5.0, 7.0]

plt.plot(years, sic_rev)

plt.ylabel("€Bn / $M")

chart_path = "/mnt/data/cagr_revenue_bridge.png"
plt.savefig(chart_path)
plt.close()

doc = SimpleDocTemplate(pdf_path, pagesize=A4)

story.append(Spacer(1, 12))

- Deployments now measured in **tens of thousands of devices**
- Buyers prioritize:
  - Safety
  - Compliance
  - Power efficiency
  - Trusted chain-of-custody
- Example: DHL SmartULD deployment (~30,000 units, first phase)

**Conclusion:** This is now an **operational market**, not experimental.

**Assistant**  _2026-01-26 14:34_

It refers to **small structural tweaks** that increase reach and visibility on LinkedIn without changing the substance of the post.

### 1) Company page tagging
When you **tag the company page** (e.g., `@Silicon Craft Technology`):
- The post is more likely to be shown to:
  - SIC employees
  - Followers of the SIC company page
- The post may appear on the company’s activity feed
- It signals alignment with the company brand (important for senior hires)

**How it’s “optimized”:**
- Company name appears exactly as the official LinkedIn page name
- Used once, early in the post (not repeatedly)

---

### 2) Hashtags
Hashtags help LinkedIn categorize and distribute the post beyond your immediate network.

**Optimized hashtags means:**
- 3–5 relevant, professional hashtags
- Industry-accurate (not generic or promotional)
- Placed at the **end** of the post

**Examples relevant to your role and SIC:**
- `#Semiconductors`
- `#ICDesign`
- `#GlobalExpansion`
- `#IndustrialTechnology`
- `#ThailandTech`

These help your post surface to:
- Semiconductor professionals
- Industry recruiters and partners
- Regional and global technology audiences

---

### What it does *not* mean
- No marketing language
- No engagement bait
- No emojis
- No “excited to announce” hype
- No excessive tagging

we have a challenge with our current general term of sales agreement.  it does not cover precious metals price fluctuation, nor does it cover FX exchange fluctuation. **Assistant**  _2026-01-27 11:10_

## 1) What your current Terms cover vs what is missing

### What you already have
- A general right to adapt prices for increases in production costs on unconfirmed orders. fileciteturn8file2L1-L5  
- An FX adjustment right **only** when (a) the order currency is **not THB** and (b) the FX move is **>3%** between **Acknowledgement and delivery**, using an “Interbanking exchange rate” reference. fileciteturn8file2L1-L5  

### What is missing (and why procurement will push back)
1) **Precious metals index clause (Au/Ag/Pd/Pt):** no definition of which metals, which index, which observation date, how often adjusted, and how it shows up on invoices/quotes.  
2) **FX clause is too narrow for global customers:**  
   - limited to “currency other than THB” and only between ack and delivery  
   - no clear FX source (procurement needs a recognized published benchmark), and “Interbanking exchange rate” is ambiguous fileciteturn8file2L1-L5  
3) **No governance/process language:** how you notify, what evidence you provide, how disputes are handled, and how you prevent “double counting” (metal + FX + other cost changes).

---

## 2) Industry-accepted approach procurement teams will approve

Procurement acceptance rises sharply when the mechanism is:
- **Formula-based** (not discretionary)
- **Transparent** (published indices + defined observation dates)
- **Symmetric** (can move up or down)
- **Bounded** (thresholds, cadence, and (optionally) caps)
- **Auditable** (supporting calculation attached to invoice or available on request)

Two clean mechanisms are standard:

### A) **Index-based surcharge line items (preferred)**
Keep the base unit price stable, and add:
- **Precious Metal Surcharge (PMS)**
- **FX Adjustment (FXA)**  
This is widely accepted because procurement can track it and reconcile it.

### B) **Quarterly price reset (alternative for large LTAs)**
Reprice quarterly using the same indices. Works best for high-volume customers with stable forecasts.

---

## 3) Recommended contract updates (draft-ready language)

Below are clauses you can drop into **Section 3 (Prices)** as sub-clauses. They are written to be procurement-friendly and to reduce negotiation cycles.

### 3.1 Definitions (add)
- **“Base Currency”**: the currency stated in the Acknowledgement.  
- **“FX Reference Rate”**: a published benchmark rate agreed by the parties (examples below).  
- **“Reference Metal Price”**: the published benchmark price for each applicable metal (examples below).  
- **“Baseline Date”**: the date used to set the baseline FX and baseline metal prices (typically quotation date or Acknowledgement date; pick one policy and apply consistently).

### 3.2 Precious Metals Price Adjustment (add)
**(a) Applicability**  
If the Goods include materials whose cost is materially affected by **gold, silver, palladium, or platinum**, SIC may apply a **Precious Metals Surcharge (PMS)**.

**(b) Benchmark and observation**  
PMS is calculated using **published benchmark prices** (to be specified in the Acknowledgement or quotation). Typical procurement-accepted benchmarks include:
- **LBMA** (gold/silver)  
- **LME** or **COMEX** (for applicable metals)  
(Choose one set and standardize.)

**(c) Calculation method (transparent)**  
PMS per unit = Σ [ (Metal Price(Current) − Metal Price(Baseline)) × Metal Content Factor ]  
- “Metal Content Factor” is SIC’s pre-agreed coefficient per part number (can be disclosed as a factor without disclosing full BOM).

**(d) Cadence and threshold**  
- Applied **monthly or quarterly** (pick one)  
- Only applied when the change vs baseline exceeds **±X%** (recommend 3–5% to align with your existing FX threshold concept).

**(e) Symmetry**  
If benchmark prices decrease, PMS decreases accordingly (procurement will often require this).

**(f) Documentation**  
SIC provides, on request, the benchmark reference, observation date, and calculation summary.

### 3.3 FX Adjustment (replace/upgrade your current clause)
Your current wording is limited and ambiguous on FX source. fileciteturn8file2L1-L5  
Replace it with:

**(a) Trigger**  
If the FX Reference Rate between the Base Currency and SIC’s cost base changes by more than **±X%** (recommend 3%) from Baseline Date to the pricing observation date, SIC may apply an **FX Adjustment (FXA)**.

**(b) Reference rate**  
FX Reference Rate will be the **published** rate agreed by the parties (examples procurement commonly accepts):
- **WM/Reuters** closing spot
- **European Central Bank** reference rates (for applicable pairs)
- A named major bank published rate (less preferred than the above)

**(c) Application**  
FXA can be applied:
- **at shipment/invoice date** for spot/short lead time orders, or
- **monthly/quarterly** for LTAs (recommended for predictability)

**(d) Symmetry**  
FXA increases or decreases with FX movements.

**(e) No double counting**  
If the contract price is already explicitly set in a currency that matches SIC’s underlying cost base for the relevant cost elements, FXA is not applied to those elements.

### 3.4 Governance / Notice (add)
- SIC provides **30 days notice** before implementing a new PMS/FXA cadence on existing LTAs, unless already defined in the Acknowledgement.  
- Disputes are handled by providing source screenshots/links and a calculation sheet; payments remain due per standard terms while disputes are resolved (procurement usually accepts this if the method is objective).

---

## 4) Implementation plan (what to put in place so customers accept it)

### Step 1 — Commercial policy (1 page internal)
- Decide: **monthly vs quarterly** for PMS and FXA (I recommend **quarterly for LTAs**, **shipment-date for spot orders**)  
- Set thresholds: recommend **±3% FX**, **±3–5% metals**
- Define the “Baseline Date” rule (quote date or acknowledgement date) and stick to it

### Step 2 — Standard “Index Exhibit” (customer-facing attachment)
Create an exhibit that lists:
- FX source and time
- Metal benchmarks
- Observation schedule
- Example calculation (with dummy numbers)
- A statement that coefficients are by part number and available under NDA if needed

### Step 3 — Quote and Acknowledgement template update
Add two visible lines:
- **PMS: [method/cadence/baseline]**
- **FXA: [method/cadence/baseline]**

This avoids procurement claiming “not agreed.”

### Step 4 — ERP / invoicing mechanics
- Add two surcharge line items to invoices: **PMS** and **FXA**  
- Store the benchmark data used (date, rate, source) per invoice for audit

### Step 5 — Customer rollout (segmented)
- Tier 1 strategic accounts: negotiate as part of LTA renewal  
- Standard accounts: introduce as a revised T&Cs annex with 30-day notice (your Terms already allow amendments with notice mechanics). fileciteturn8file4L18-L24  

---

## 5) One critical note to align internally
Your Terms are governed by **Singapore law and Singapore courts**. fileciteturn8file0L26-L35  
That is fine, but once you introduce PMS/FXA, customers may ask for:
- clearer definitions,
- symmetry (downward adjustments), and
- documented indices.

If you adopt the structure above, those objections are largely removed.

**Assistant**  _2026-01-27 11:16_

Below is a **clear, procurement-ready answer**, followed by **draft contract language** aligned to your choices and **industry standards**. This is written so it can be dropped directly into your General Terms or used as a redline.

---

## 1) Governing law: SG law vs ICC vs “international”

### Short answer (industry reality)
**Keep Singapore law. Do NOT replace it with ICC.**

### Why
- **ICC is not a governing law**. The International Chamber of Commerce provides:
  - Arbitration rules
  - Incoterms  
  It does **not** function as a legal system for contracts.
- Procurement teams expect:
  - A **recognized national law**
  - A **neutral, business-friendly jurisdiction**
- **Singapore law** is:
  - One of the top two global choices (alongside English law)
  - Extremely common in semiconductor, electronics, and global supply contracts
  - Seen as neutral, predictable, and enforceable worldwide

### Best practice (what global customers accept)
- **Governing law:** Singapore law  
- **Dispute resolution:** Singapore courts *or* SIAC arbitration (optional, not required)

👉 **Recommendation:**  
**Keep Singapore law exactly as-is.**  
Do not introduce ICC as governing law; that would raise red flags with procurement and legal.

---

## 2) FX benchmark: global standard to use

### Best global, procurement-accepted benchmark
**WM/Reuters Closing Spot Rate**

### Why WM/Reuters
- Used by:
  - Multinationals
  - Banks
  - Big 4 auditors
- Daily, transparent, auditable
- Accepted globally across:
  - US, EU, Asia
- Far more precise than “interbank rate” (which is ambiguous)

### Recommendation
Use:
> **WM/Reuters Closing Spot Rate as published on the last business day of the applicable quarter**

This is the cleanest, least disputed choice.

---

## 3) Metals to include (industry-correct)

Your list is correct and defensible.

### Recommended metals (standard for IC / wafer manufacturing)
- **Gold (Au)** – bonding, plating
- **Copper (Cu)** – interconnects, leadframes
- **Aluminum (Al)** – metallization, packaging
- **Silver (Ag)** – plating, specialty interconnects
- **Platinum (Pt)** – specialty processes, sensors

### Optional (only if applicable in your BOMs)
- **Palladium (Pd)** – plating, specialty components

👉 **Important:**  
Procurement will accept this **if**:
- Adjustment is **formula-based**
- Coefficients are **pre-defined by part number**
- SIC does **not disclose BOM**, only a metal factor

---

## 4) Final recommended structure (what procurement will accept)

- **Quarterly adjustment**
- **Baseline = Quote date**
- **Symmetric (up and down)**
- **Transparent indices**
- **Separate surcharge lines** (not baked into unit price)

This aligns with semiconductor industry norms (foundries, OSATs, specialty IC suppliers).

---

## 5) Draft clause language (redline-ready)

### 5.1 Definitions (add to Section 1)

**“Baseline Date”** means the quotation date stated in SIC’s quotation.  

**“FX Reference Rate”** means the WM/Reuters Closing Spot Rate published on the last business day of the applicable quarter.  

**“Reference Metal Prices”** means the officially published benchmark prices for the applicable metals, including Gold (Au), Copper (Cu), Aluminum (Al), Silver (Ag), Platinum (Pt), and other critical materials used in wafer and IC manufacturing, as specified below.

---

### 5.2 Precious Metals and Materials Price Adjustment (Quarterly)

(a) **Applicability**  
Where the Goods include materials whose cost is materially affected by changes in the prices of Gold, Copper, Aluminum, Silver, Platinum, or other critical materials used in wafer and IC manufacturing, SIC may apply a **Materials Price Adjustment (MPA)**.

(b) **Benchmark prices**  
The MPA shall be calculated using the following published benchmarks, as applicable:
- **Gold / Silver / Platinum:** LBMA  
- **Copper / Aluminum:** London Metal Exchange (LME)

(c) **Calculation method**  
MPA per unit shall be calculated quarterly based on the difference between:
- the Reference Metal Prices on the Baseline Date, and
- the Reference Metal Prices on the last business day of the applicable quarter,

multiplied by a pre-defined material coefficient applicable to the relevant part number.

(d) **Threshold**  
MPA shall apply only where the cumulative change in the applicable Reference Metal Prices exceeds **±3%** from the Baseline Date.

(e) **Symmetry**  
MPA shall increase or decrease in accordance with increases or decreases in Reference Metal Prices.

(f) **Transparency**  
Upon reasonable request, SIC shall provide the applicable benchmark references, observation dates, and a summary calculation.

---

### 5.3 Foreign Exchange Adjustment (Quarterly)

(a) **Applicability**  
Where SIC’s underlying cost base is affected by foreign exchange movements, SIC may apply a **Foreign Exchange Adjustment (FXA)**.

(b) **Benchmark**  
FXA shall be calculated using the **WM/Reuters Closing Spot Rate**.

(c) **Calculation**  
FXA shall be determined quarterly based on the percentage change between:
- the FX Reference Rate on the Baseline Date, and
- the FX Reference Rate on the last business day of the applicable quarter.

(d) **Threshold**  
FXA shall apply only where the FX movement exceeds **±3%**.

(e) **Symmetry**  
FXA shall increase or decrease in accordance with FX movements.

(f) **No double counting**  
FXA shall not apply to cost elements already denominated in the same currency as the agreed Base Currency.

---

### 5.4 Invoicing and Notice

- MPA and FXA shall be shown as **separate line items** on invoices.  
- Adjustments shall be applied **quarterly**.  
- SIC shall provide **advance notice** of the applicable adjustment cycle upon contract execution or renewal.

---

- **Quarterly cadence** = predictable budgeting
- **Symmetric** (not one-sided)
- **No discretionary pricing**
- **Auditable and transparent**
- Mirrors how:
  - Foundries
  - OSATs
  - Specialty semiconductor suppliers manage volatility

## 2) Final contract language (ready to insert / redline)

### 2.1 Definitions (add)

**“Baseline Date”** means the quotation date stated in Seller’s quotation.

**“FX Reference Rate”** means the WM/Reuters Closing Spot Rate published on the last business day of the applicable calendar quarter.

**“Reference Metal Prices”** means the officially published benchmark prices for Gold (Au), Copper (Cu), Aluminum (Al), Silver (Ag), Platinum (Pt), and other critical materials used in wafer and integrated circuit manufacturing, as applicable.

---

### 2.2 Materials Price Adjustment (MPA)

(a) **Applicability**  
Where the Goods include materials whose cost is materially affected by changes in the prices of Gold, Copper, Aluminum, Silver, Platinum, or other critical materials used in wafer and integrated circuit manufacturing, Seller may apply a **Materials Price Adjustment (“MPA”)**.

(b) **Benchmark prices**  
MPA shall be calculated using the following published benchmarks:
- **Gold, Silver, Platinum:** LBMA published prices  
- **Copper, Aluminum:** London Metal Exchange (LME) published prices

(c) **Calculation**  
MPA per unit shall be calculated on a **quarterly basis** based on the difference between:
- the applicable Reference Metal Prices on the Baseline Date, and  
- the applicable Reference Metal Prices on the last business day of the applicable quarter,  

multiplied by a pre-defined material coefficient applicable to the relevant part number.

(d) **Threshold**  
MPA shall apply only where the cumulative change in the applicable Reference Metal Prices exceeds **±3%** compared to the Baseline Date.

(e) **Symmetry**  
MPA shall increase or decrease in accordance with increases or decreases in the applicable Reference Metal Prices.

(f) **Transparency**  
Upon reasonable request, Seller shall provide the applicable benchmark references, observation dates, and a summary of the calculation.

---

### 2.3 Foreign Exchange Adjustment (FXA)

(a) **Applicability**  
Where Seller’s underlying cost base is affected by foreign exchange movements, Seller may apply a **Foreign Exchange Adjustment (“FXA”)**.

(b) **Benchmark**  
FXA shall be calculated using the **FX Reference Rate**.

(c) **Calculation**  
FXA shall be determined on a **quarterly basis** based on the percentage change between:
- the FX Reference Rate on the Baseline Date, and  
- the FX Reference Rate on the last business day of the applicable quarter.

(d) **Threshold**  
FXA shall apply only where the foreign exchange movement exceeds **±3%** compared to the Baseline Date.

(e) **Symmetry**  
FXA shall increase or decrease in accordance with favorable or unfavorable foreign exchange movements.

(f) **No double counting**  
FXA shall not apply to cost elements already denominated in the same currency as the agreed Base Currency.

---

### 2.4 Invoicing and Notice

- Any MPA and FXA shall be shown as **separate line items** on Seller’s invoices.  
- Adjustments shall be applied **quarterly**.  
- The adjustment methodology shall be deemed agreed upon acceptance of Seller’s quotation or order acknowledgement.

# DOCUMENT 1  
## **Silicon Craft Technology  
Materials Price Adjustment (MPA) and Foreign Exchange Adjustment (FXA)  
Contract Addendum (Redline Text)**

### 1. Definitions

For the purposes of these General Terms of Sale:

1.1 **“Baseline Date”** means the quotation date stated in Seller’s quotation.

1.2 **“Base Currency”** means the currency stated in Seller’s quotation or order acknowledgement.

1.3 **“FX Reference Rate”** means the WM/Reuters Closing Spot Rate published on the last business day of the applicable calendar quarter.

1.4 **“Reference Metal Prices”** means the officially published benchmark prices for Gold (Au), Copper (Cu), Aluminum (Al), Silver (Ag), Platinum (Pt), and other critical materials used in wafer and integrated circuit manufacturing, as applicable.

---

### 2. Materials Price Adjustment (MPA)

2.1 **Applicability**  
Where the Goods include materials whose cost is materially affected by changes in the prices of Gold (Au), Copper (Cu), Aluminum (Al), Silver (Ag), Platinum (Pt), or other critical materials used in wafer and integrated circuit manufacturing, Seller may apply a Materials Price Adjustment (“MPA”) in accordance with this Section.

2.2 **Benchmark Prices**  
The MPA shall be calculated using the following published benchmark prices, as applicable:

(a) Gold (Au), Silver (Ag), Platinum (Pt): London Bullion Market Association (LBMA) published prices;  
(b) Copper (Cu), Aluminum (Al): London Metal Exchange (LME) published prices.

2.3 **Calculation Method**  
The MPA shall be calculated on a quarterly basis by comparing:

(a) the applicable Reference Metal Prices on the Baseline Date; and  
(b) the applicable Reference Metal Prices on the last business day of the applicable calendar quarter,

and multiplying the difference by a pre-defined material coefficient applicable to the relevant part number.

2.4 **Threshold**  
The MPA shall apply only where the cumulative change in the applicable Reference Metal Prices exceeds plus or minus three percent (±3%) compared to the Baseline Date.

2.5 **Symmetry**  
The MPA shall increase or decrease in accordance with increases or decreases in the applicable Reference Metal Prices.

2.6 **Transparency**  
Upon reasonable request, Seller shall provide the applicable benchmark references, observation dates, and a summary of the calculation of the MPA.

---

### 3. Foreign Exchange Adjustment (FXA)

3.1 **Applicability**  
Where Seller’s underlying cost base is affected by foreign exchange movements, Seller may apply a Foreign Exchange Adjustment (“FXA”) in accordance with this Section.

3.2 **Benchmark**  
The FXA shall be calculated using the FX Reference Rate.

3.3 **Calculation Method**  
The FXA shall be calculated on a quarterly basis by comparing:

(a) the FX Reference Rate on the Baseline Date; and  
(b) the FX Reference Rate on the last business day of the applicable calendar quarter.

3.4 **Threshold**  
The FXA shall apply only where the foreign exchange movement exceeds plus or minus three percent (±3%) compared to the Baseline Date.

3.5 **Symmetry**  
The FXA shall increase or decrease in accordance with favorable or unfavorable foreign exchange movements.

3.6 **No Double Counting**  
The FXA shall not apply to cost elements already denominated in the same currency as the Base Currency.

---

### 4. Invoicing and Application

4.1 Any MPA and FXA shall be shown as **separate line items** on Seller’s invoices.

4.2 MPA and FXA shall be applied on a **quarterly basis**.

4.3 Acceptance of Seller’s quotation or order acknowledgement shall constitute Buyer’s acceptance of the MPA and FXA methodology described herein.

---

### 5. Governing Law and Jurisdiction

5.1 This Addendum and any disputes arising out of or in connection with it shall be governed by and construed in accordance with the laws of **Singapore**.

5.2 The courts of **Singapore** shall have exclusive jurisdiction.

---

---

# DOCUMENT 2  
## **Silicon Craft Technology  
Materials and Foreign Exchange Adjustment Exhibit  
(Customer-Facing Explanation)**

### 1. Purpose

This Exhibit explains the transparent, index-based mechanism used by Silicon Craft Technology (“SIC”) to manage raw material and foreign exchange volatility in a fair, auditable, and industry-standard manner.

---

### 2. Key Principles

- Adjustments are **index-based**, not discretionary  
- Adjustments are reviewed on a **quarterly basis**  
- Publicly available benchmarks are used  
- Adjustments are **symmetric** (may increase or decrease)  
- Adjustments are shown as **separate line items** on invoices  
- No bill of materials disclosure is required  

---

### 3. Materials Covered

The Materials Price Adjustment (“MPA”) may apply to Goods that include materials whose costs are materially affected by changes in the prices of:

- Gold (Au)  
- Copper (Cu)  
- Aluminum (Al)  
- Silver (Ag)  
- Platinum (Pt)  

and other critical materials used in wafer and integrated circuit manufacturing, where applicable.

---

### 4. Materials Price Adjustment (MPA)

4.1 **Benchmark Sources**  
The following published benchmarks are used:

- Gold, Silver, Platinum: London Bullion Market Association (LBMA)  
- Copper, Aluminum: London Metal Exchange (LME)

4.2 **Baseline**  
The baseline for calculation is the **quotation date**.

4.3 **Calculation**  
The MPA is calculated quarterly based on the difference between:

- the applicable benchmark price on the quotation date; and  
- the applicable benchmark price on the last business day of the applicable quarter,

multiplied by a pre-defined material coefficient applicable to the relevant part number.

4.4 **Threshold**  
The MPA applies only when the cumulative price change exceeds **±3%**.

4.5 **Symmetry**  
If benchmark prices decrease, the MPA decreases accordingly.

---

### 5. Foreign Exchange Adjustment (FXA)

5.1 **Benchmark Source**  
Foreign exchange adjustments are calculated using the **WM/Reuters Closing Spot Rate**.

5.2 **Baseline**  
The baseline for FX calculation is the **quotation date**.

5.3 **Calculation**  
The FXA is calculated quarterly based on the percentage change between:

- the WM/Reuters Closing Spot Rate on the quotation date; and  
- the WM/Reuters Closing Spot Rate on the last business day of the applicable quarter.

5.4 **Threshold**  
The FXA applies only when the cumulative foreign exchange movement exceeds **±3%**.

5.5 **Symmetry**  
FX adjustments may increase or decrease in accordance with currency movements.

---

### 6. Invoicing and Transparency

- MPA and FXA are shown as **separate line items** on invoices.  
- Upon reasonable request, SIC can provide benchmark references, observation dates, and a summary calculation.  

---

### 7. Summary

This approach reflects standard practices used across the global semiconductor industry and is designed to ensure pricing remains fair, transparent, and sustainable for both parties.

Below is a **formal legal-style document**, written as it would appear in a General Terms of Sale or as a contractual addendum.  
Numbered sections, precise language, conservative tone.  
You can **copy/paste this verbatim** into your agreement or redline.

---

# **ADDENDUM TO GENERAL TERMS OF SALE**  
## **MATERIALS PRICE ADJUSTMENT AND FOREIGN EXCHANGE ADJUSTMENT**

This Addendum forms part of and shall be read together with the General Terms of Sale (“Terms”) of **Silicon Craft Technology Public Company Limited** (“Seller”). In the event of any conflict, this Addendum shall prevail with respect to the subject matter herein.

---

## 1. DEFINITIONS

For the purposes of these Terms, the following definitions shall apply:

1.1 **“Baseline Date”** means the quotation date stated in Seller’s written quotation.

1.2 **“Base Currency”** means the currency specified in Seller’s quotation or order acknowledgement.

1.3 **“FX Reference Rate”** means the WM/Reuters Closing Spot Rate published on the last business day of the applicable calendar quarter.

1.4 **“Reference Metal Prices”** means the officially published benchmark prices for Gold (Au), Copper (Cu), Aluminum (Al), Silver (Ag), Platinum (Pt), and other critical materials used in wafer and integrated circuit manufacturing, as applicable.

---

## 2. MATERIALS PRICE ADJUSTMENT (MPA)

2.1 **Applicability**  
Where the Goods include materials whose cost is materially affected by fluctuations in the prices of Gold (Au), Copper (Cu), Aluminum (Al), Silver (Ag), Platinum (Pt), or other critical materials used in wafer and integrated circuit manufacturing, Seller shall be entitled to apply a materials price adjustment (“Materials Price Adjustment” or “MPA”) in accordance with this Section 2.

2.2 **Benchmark Sources**  
The MPA shall be calculated using the following publicly available benchmark prices, as applicable:

(a) Gold (Au), Silver (Ag), Platinum (Pt): prices published by the London Bullion Market Association (LBMA);  
(b) Copper (Cu), Aluminum (Al): prices published by the London Metal Exchange (LME).

2.3 **Calculation Methodology**  
The MPA shall be calculated on a quarterly basis by comparing:

(a) the applicable Reference Metal Prices on the Baseline Date; and  
(b) the applicable Reference Metal Prices on the last business day of the relevant calendar quarter,

and multiplying the difference by a pre-defined material coefficient applicable to the relevant part number. Such coefficient reflects the relative contribution of the applicable material to the cost of the Goods.

2.4 **Threshold**  
The MPA shall apply only where the cumulative change in the applicable Reference Metal Prices exceeds plus or minus three percent (±3%) relative to the Reference Metal Prices on the Baseline Date.

2.5 **Symmetry**  
The MPA shall apply symmetrically. In the event that Reference Metal Prices decrease, the MPA shall decrease accordingly.

2.6 **Transparency**  
Upon reasonable request, Seller shall provide Buyer with the applicable benchmark references, observation dates, and a summary of the MPA calculation. Seller shall not be required to disclose its bill of materials or cost breakdown.

---

## 3. FOREIGN EXCHANGE ADJUSTMENT (FXA)

3.1 **Applicability**  
Where Seller’s underlying cost base is materially affected by fluctuations in foreign exchange rates, Seller shall be entitled to apply a foreign exchange adjustment (“Foreign Exchange Adjustment” or “FXA”) in accordance with this Section 3.

3.2 **Benchmark**  
The FXA shall be calculated using the FX Reference Rate.

3.3 **Calculation Methodology**  
The FXA shall be calculated on a quarterly basis by comparing:

(a) the FX Reference Rate on the Baseline Date; and  
(b) the FX Reference Rate on the last business day of the relevant calendar quarter.

3.4 **Threshold**  
The FXA shall apply only where the cumulative foreign exchange movement exceeds plus or minus three percent (±3%) relative to the FX Reference Rate on the Baseline Date.

3.5 **Symmetry**  
The FXA shall apply symmetrically. Favorable or unfavorable foreign exchange movements shall result in corresponding decreases or increases in the FXA.

3.6 **No Double Counting**  
The FXA shall not apply to cost elements already denominated in the Base Currency.

---

## 4. APPLICATION AND INVOICING

4.1 Any MPA and FXA applied pursuant to this Addendum shall be calculated on a quarterly basis.

4.2 Any applicable MPA and FXA shall be clearly identified as **separate line items** on Seller’s invoices.

4.3 Acceptance of Seller’s quotation, order acknowledgement, or delivery of the Goods shall constitute Buyer’s acceptance of the MPA and FXA mechanisms described in this Addendum.

---

## 5. GOVERNING LAW AND JURISDICTION

5.1 This Addendum and any dispute arising out of or in connection with it shall be governed by and construed in accordance with the laws of **Singapore**, without regard to its conflict of laws principles.

5.2 The courts of **Singapore** shall have exclusive jurisdiction over any dispute arising out of or in connection with this Addendum.

---

## 6. CONTINUITY

6.1 Except as expressly amended by this Addendum, all other terms and conditions of the Terms shall remain in full force and effect.

Below is a **formal legal justification memorandum**, written in the style typically shared with **customer legal and procurement teams**.  
It explains *why* the clauses are reasonable, standard, and enforceable, without being argumentative.

You can copy/paste this verbatim and brand it as needed.

---

# **LEGAL JUSTIFICATION MEMORANDUM**  
## **Materials Price Adjustment (MPA) and Foreign Exchange Adjustment (FXA)**

**Issuer:** Silicon Craft Technology Public Company Limited  
**Purpose:** Legal and commercial justification of Materials Price Adjustment and Foreign Exchange Adjustment provisions  
**Applicable Law:** Singapore law  
**Audience:** Customer Legal, Procurement, and Commercial Teams  

---

## 1. Purpose of This Memorandum

This memorandum explains the legal and commercial rationale for the Materials Price Adjustment (“MPA”) and Foreign Exchange Adjustment (“FXA”) provisions included in Seller’s General Terms of Sale. These provisions are designed to ensure pricing fairness, transparency, and sustainability in light of objectively measurable cost volatility inherent in semiconductor manufacturing.

---

## 2. Industry Context

The semiconductor industry is subject to significant fluctuations in:

- Precious and base metal prices used in wafer fabrication, packaging, and assembly processes; and  
- Foreign exchange rates affecting cross-border manufacturing, sourcing, and logistics.

It is standard practice across the global semiconductor, electronics, and advanced manufacturing industries for suppliers to address such volatility through index-based adjustment mechanisms rather than discretionary price changes. Comparable mechanisms are commonly used by foundries, OSAT providers, specialty IC suppliers, and advanced materials manufacturers.

---

## 3. Legal Basis Under Singapore Law

Under Singapore law, contractual price adjustment mechanisms are enforceable provided that they are:

- Clearly defined;  
- Based on objective and verifiable criteria; and  
- Agreed in advance by the contracting parties.

The MPA and FXA provisions satisfy these requirements by:
- Defining the applicable benchmarks in advance;
- Using publicly available, independent indices; and
- Applying adjustments in a formula-based and non-discretionary manner.

Singapore courts generally uphold such mechanisms where the adjustment methodology is transparent and mutually agreed at contract formation.

---

## 4. Materials Price Adjustment (MPA) — Legal Justification

### 4.1 Objective Benchmarks
The MPA relies exclusively on internationally recognized and publicly available benchmarks, including:
- London Bullion Market Association (LBMA) prices; and
- London Metal Exchange (LME) prices.

These benchmarks are widely accepted by global procurement and legal teams as objective measures of raw material price movements.

### 4.2 Defined Scope
The MPA applies only to materials that are demonstrably relevant to wafer and integrated circuit manufacturing, including Gold, Copper, Aluminum, Silver, and Platinum. The mechanism does not permit arbitrary inclusion of unrelated materials.

### 4.3 Threshold and Symmetry
The MPA applies only when price movements exceed a defined threshold (±3%) and operates symmetrically, allowing for both upward and downward adjustments. This symmetry ensures that the mechanism is not one-sided and supports commercial fairness.

### 4.4 No Disclosure of Proprietary Information
The use of pre-defined material coefficients allows adjustment calculations without requiring disclosure of Seller’s bill of materials or proprietary cost structures, which is consistent with industry norms and intellectual property protection principles.

---

## 5. Foreign Exchange Adjustment (FXA) — Legal Justification

### 5.1 Use of Recognized FX Benchmark
The FXA is calculated using the WM/Reuters Closing Spot Rate, a globally recognized and widely audited benchmark used by financial institutions, multinational corporations, and auditors.

### 5.2 Predictable Application
FXA is applied on a quarterly basis with a defined threshold (±3%), allowing Buyers to anticipate potential adjustments and plan accordingly. This predictability supports procurement budgeting and financial forecasting.

### 5.3 Prevention of Double Counting
The FXA expressly excludes cost elements already denominated in the Base Currency, ensuring that adjustments reflect genuine exposure to foreign exchange risk and do not result in duplicative pricing effects.

---

## 6. Transparency and Auditability

Both MPA and FXA mechanisms provide for transparency by allowing Buyers, upon reasonable request, to review:
- The applicable benchmark references;
- Observation dates; and
- Summary calculation methodologies.

This level of transparency is consistent with best practices in global supply contracts and supports good faith commercial dealings.

---

## 7. Alignment with International Commercial Practice

The structure and substance of the MPA and FXA provisions are aligned with widely accepted international commercial practices, including:
- Index-based pricing mechanisms;
- Periodic review cycles; and
- Clear governance and invoicing disclosure.

Such mechanisms are commonly accepted by procurement and legal teams in the semiconductor, electronics, automotive, and industrial sectors.

---

## 8. Conclusion

The Materials Price Adjustment and Foreign Exchange Adjustment provisions are reasonable, balanced, and legally enforceable under Singapore law. They reflect established industry standards, rely on objective and transparent benchmarks, and ensure long-term pricing sustainability without introducing discretionary or unpredictable pricing behavior.

These provisions are intended to protect both parties from uncontrollable market volatility while preserving commercial fairness and contractual certainty.

---

# **LEGAL JUSTIFICATION MEMORANDUM**  
## **Materials Price Adjustment (MPA) and Foreign Exchange Adjustment (FXA)**

**Issuer:** Silicon Craft Technology Public Company Limited  
**Purpose:** Legal and commercial justification of Materials Price Adjustment and Foreign Exchange Adjustment provisions  
**Applicable Law:** Singapore law  
**Audience:** Customer Legal, Procurement, and Commercial Teams  

---

## 1. Purpose of This Memorandum

This memorandum explains the legal and commercial rationale for the Materials Price Adjustment (“MPA”) and Foreign Exchange Adjustment (“FXA”) provisions included in Seller’s General Terms of Sale. These provisions are designed to ensure pricing fairness, transparency, and sustainability in light of objectively measurable cost volatility inherent in semiconductor manufacturing.

Such mechanisms are widely used in long-term supply agreements in the semiconductor, electronics, and advanced manufacturing industries.

---

## 2. Industry Context

The semiconductor industry is subject to significant fluctuations in:

- Precious and base metal prices used in wafer fabrication, packaging, bonding, metallization, and assembly processes; and  
- Foreign exchange rates affecting cross-border manufacturing, sourcing, and logistics.

Index-based price adjustment mechanisms are standard practice across the industry and are commonly implemented by foundries, OSATs, specialty IC suppliers, and advanced materials manufacturers. These mechanisms are favored over discretionary repricing because they provide predictability, transparency, and auditability.

**Industry reference examples include:**
- Foundry and OSAT long-term agreements incorporating metal and FX adjustment clauses  
- Electronics supply contracts referencing LBMA, LME, and WM/Reuters benchmarks  
- Global procurement frameworks used by Tier-1 OEMs and semiconductor manufacturers  

---

## 3. Legal Basis Under Singapore Law

Under Singapore law, contractual price adjustment mechanisms are enforceable provided that they are:

- Clearly defined;  
- Based on objective and verifiable criteria; and  
- Agreed in advance by the contracting parties.

Singapore courts recognize and enforce index-based pricing mechanisms where the methodology is transparent and not discretionary. This approach aligns with general principles of freedom of contract and commercial certainty under Singapore law.

**Legal reference principles:**
- Singapore Contract Law – enforceability of agreed pricing formulas  
- Judicial recognition of objective benchmark-based contractual mechanisms  
- Common use of index-linked pricing in international supply contracts governed by Singapore law  

---

## 4. Materials Price Adjustment (MPA) — Legal Justification

### 4.1 Objective Benchmarks

The MPA relies exclusively on internationally recognized, independent, and publicly available benchmarks:

- **London Bullion Market Association (LBMA)** — widely used global reference for gold, silver, and platinum pricing  
- **London Metal Exchange (LME)** — global reference for copper and aluminum pricing

These benchmarks are routinely referenced in commercial supply agreements across the semiconductor, electronics, automotive, and industrial sectors.

**Benchmark references:**
- LBMA official price publications  
- LME official daily settlement prices  

---

### 4.2 Defined Scope

The MPA applies only to materials demonstrably relevant to wafer and integrated circuit manufacturing, including:

- Gold (Au)  
- Copper (Cu)  
- Aluminum (Al)  
- Silver (Ag)  
- Platinum (Pt)  

This scope reflects common materials used in IC fabrication, packaging, bonding, and metallization processes and avoids arbitrary or unrelated cost elements.

---

### 4.3 Threshold and Symmetry

The MPA applies only when cumulative benchmark price movements exceed a defined threshold (±3%) and applies symmetrically to both increases and decreases.

This structure:
- Prevents minor price fluctuations from triggering frequent adjustments  
- Ensures the mechanism is balanced and commercially fair  
- Aligns with procurement expectations for index-based adjustments  

Symmetric mechanisms are widely viewed by procurement and legal teams as reasonable and non-punitive.

---

### 4.4 Protection of Proprietary Information

The use of pre-defined material coefficients allows calculation of adjustments without disclosure of Seller’s bill of materials or cost breakdown.

This approach is consistent with:
- Intellectual property protection norms in the semiconductor industry  
- Confidentiality obligations in global supply agreements  
- Standard commercial practice among foundries and specialty IC suppliers  

---

## 5. Foreign Exchange Adjustment (FXA) — Legal Justification

### 5.1 Use of Recognized FX Benchmark

The FXA relies on the **WM/Reuters Closing Spot Rate**, which is:

- One of the most widely used FX benchmarks globally  
- Referenced by multinational corporations, banks, auditors, and regulators  
- Considered objective, transparent, and independently verifiable  

**Benchmark reference:**
- Refinitiv / WM (formerly WM/Reuters) FX benchmark publications  

---

### 5.2 Predictable and Bounded Application

The FXA is applied:
- On a **quarterly basis**  
- Only when FX movements exceed a defined threshold (±3%)  

This ensures predictability for Buyers and supports budget planning and cost forecasting, consistent with global procurement standards.

---

### 5.3 Prevention of Double Counting

The FXA expressly excludes cost elements already denominated in the Base Currency. This ensures the adjustment reflects genuine foreign exchange exposure and does not result in duplicative pricing effects.

Such exclusions are standard in international supply contracts and are commonly required by procurement teams.

---

## 6. Transparency and Auditability

Both MPA and FXA mechanisms provide for transparency by allowing Buyers, upon reasonable request, to review:

- The applicable benchmark sources (LBMA, LME, WM/Reuters);  
- The observation dates used; and  
- A summary calculation of the applicable adjustment.

This level of transparency is consistent with good faith commercial practice and supports internal audit and compliance requirements on both sides.

---

## 7. Alignment with International Commercial Practice

The MPA and FXA provisions align with widely accepted international commercial practices, including:

- Index-based pricing mechanisms  
- Periodic (quarterly) review cycles  
- Use of independent, published benchmarks  
- Separate invoicing disclosure  

Such mechanisms are routinely accepted in contracts involving semiconductor manufacturing, electronics components, automotive supply chains, and industrial technology.

---

## 8. Conclusion

The Materials Price Adjustment and Foreign Exchange Adjustment provisions are reasonable, balanced, and legally enforceable under Singapore law. They rely on objective, widely recognized benchmarks, apply symmetrically, and provide transparency without requiring disclosure of proprietary information.

These provisions are intended to protect both parties from uncontrollable market volatility while preserving commercial fairness, contractual certainty, and long-term supply sustainability.

---

### External Benchmark References (for appendix or footnotes)

- London Bullion Market Association (LBMA) – Official precious metal prices  
- London Metal Exchange (LME) – Official base metal settlement prices  
- Refinitiv / WM – WM/Reuters FX Closing Spot Rates  
- Singapore Contract Law principles on enforceability of agreed pricing mechanisms
